Foreword

Tam happy to wuite these few woids by way of introduction to and apprecia-
tion of Kaviray Ambikaduttayi’s book entitled Suéruta Samhita ( enae-azaedt
frerearea-aagfan )

Susruta Samhita 1s one of the two most ancient, encyclopedic and authorita-
tive classical books of Indian Medicine It is one of the few books that have lived a
life of about two millemums. Its survival for such a long period becomes quite cons-
picuously surprising when our day to day experience is that in our modern library
of hundreds of thousands of volumes, a book 1s hardly extant for a period of even half
acentury The comparative longevity of such books can be explained if we refer
to OSLER’s classification of authors He groups them in thiee classes—creator,
transmuter and transmitter The fist is the instinctive, mspired, omginal and
intellectual type He is yare, his work remains long-hved rather eternal and
fountain-head of future generation He may be one ina million The second is of the
similar type but somewhat less in omginal all-comprehensive concept but more
practical and more worldly. He makes the knowledge of the first type more intelli-
gible, practical and useful Such persons are created in every new age or rather they
may be the creators of new age They are respected more or less equally as the first
type and are placed nearly in the same category as the first The third type is
mere copyist They se1ve the purpose of keeping the torch of knowledge burning

Sugruta Samhtta is the creation of the sage surgeon of the first type

Somebody may raise the question that there may be some older text-book
other than Sugruta Samhita, and it must be given the place of veneration The
~~ reply will rationalize our justification in bestowing the prime place to Sugruta
-. Sambita in Surgery There may be scattered surgical knowledge, experience and
practice preceding the age of compilation of Sugruta Samhita but to systematise
and put it on a scientafic level so that genoralzation and progress may be possible,
needed the learmng and experience of a creative personality of the author of
Sugruta Samhita

It is not the chronological priority that is respected but it is the scientific
superiority thats highly valued Vagbhata the author of the second type who
flourished in the fourth century also states the samo,

safaaahe ifediegren sega | NSTI: FS a gages cea set GatlraT |

‘If the works of the ancient Hisis alone'are worthy of interest, why are Bhela
and such others not studied, leaving off Charaka and gyeyuta 2 Therefore it is right
that a good work should always be accepted.’

=== PAGE 5 ===

134

The Suéruta Samiuta beng of a superb type of work, the previous books fell
into oblivion, and all the later treatises drew inspixation and guidance 1n theoretical
concepts and practical work from 16

Tt will be too presumptive to wiite a review for such a book, even a transmu-
tex can write only explanatory commentary or quote wholesale excerpts from here
and there

It is not possible for a common man hke me to fathom the depths of such a
meaningful and purposeful book I can do best to jom hands with the scholars of the
past and present age who have looked at 1 with all admiration and as guiding star
and spirit, in the progress of science for the benefit of the humanity.

sThe present commentator 1s a person of wide expenence and erudition. Asa
result of indefatiguable industry and diligence he has been able to compile and
publish a number of books on various branches of Ayurveda In this compilation he
has taken great pains to collect the vaiious scholarly commentaries and tied to
compare and contrast them with one clear purpose that 1s to elucidate the real

meaning and best interpretation of the text. Charaka lays equal stress on mterpre-
tation as on the study of the text alone.

sorfaraists arent aeageear fran fraa | anftereate aerating wae TAT UI

‘A physician who is not conversant wsth the canons of exposition, though he
may be a student of many treatises, will fail to grasp the meaning of these treatases,
just as a man fails to acyune wealth when fortune has deserted him’

Here too I find myself not competent enough to give fall justice to the labour
of the learned author.

A number of commentators in different periods of the last fifteen hundred

years have commented on 16 according to their predilection It has been translated
in various languages of the East and West

The question now arises as to what is the use of adding one more translation
and commentary to already extant and available scores of such compilations The
need and justification of such a new publication will be easily undeistood and

realized if we study the present circumstances of establishment of Renaissance
- of, Ayurveda

THE PRESENT AGH IS THE PERIOD OF RENAISSANCE AND
RE-EMERGENCE OF AYURVEDA

In the end of the last’
to the realization of the val
study of Sansknit by Weste

eee India awoke from her long slumber. She awoke
Ue of her heritage m art, lterature and Phylog

2 ophy. The
m Savants, by the Royal Asiatic Society and other

=== PAGE 6 ===

[3 j

bodies of men of learning and enlightenment was in a great measure responsible for
this renaissance As a result of the impact between her age-old culture and the new
forces of modern thought from the West, India became conscious of her mission and
Special contribution to the world in the matter of thought, way of life and spiritual
ideals The inadequacies of modern scientific mode of life and thought became
gradually evident and the people of the west themselves realized that mere scientific
achievement was going to lead mankind nowhere The literature and philosophy of
ancient India is full of imspiration for moral and spiritual upliftment of man The
emphasis is all on the psychic and spiritual aspects, the unseen in man As medicine
is an integral part of the sacred literature of the Vedas, the scholars’ and resear-
chers’ eye fell on that and one after another of these scholars beginning with Max
Muller right up to Sylvain Levi and Dr. Filliozat of the West and our own learned
Pandits, Vaidyas and Historians of philosophy like Dr. Radhakrishnan and Das-
gupta discovered the unique achievement of India in Medical thought and practice.
Indian Medicine has since been the subject of study of many onentalists like
Zimmer, Julius Jolly, Sigerist and other famous savants

As a part of the new national awakemmg which demanded political emancipa-
tion there arose also the desire for resuscitating the age-old system of Medicine
and for adjusting it to the new conditions Consequently year after year conferences
were held for the upliftment of Ayurveda and the persistent demand for sucha
revival persuaded even the British Government to open schools of medicine and
give some degree of encouragement to Indian Medicine

With the achievement of Independence, the people of India desired more
than ever to revive the age-old system of Ayurveda and by methods of modern
research to interpret it in terms intelligible to the modern world and wherever
necessary import new trends into Ayurvedic treatment and drugs without
endangering its basic structure and pattern.

As a consequence of this the various states mstituted courses of study
embodying the whole Ayurveda and the basic knowledge of Modern Medicine The
Renaissance is still taking shape and it is hoped 16 will emerge fully in the form of
a synthetic or integrated system of Universal Medicine wherein the contribution
of Ayurveda will be fully embodied for the World’s benefit

PARALLELISM OF THE RENAISSANCE OF AYURVEDA WITH
THAT OF MODERN SCIENCE ~

The period through which India is passing at present may well be compared
with the beginning of the Scientific Age of Europe. There runs a great parallelism
between the development of Ayurveda and that of western medical science. In 460
B. C, Hippocrates of Cos, who is called the father of the modern medicine, collected
the medieal knowledge of his times and codified it in the scientific methods, just as

=== PAGE 7 ===

[4]

Charaka did in India. Later during the Roman ascendancy, Galen of Pergamon
wrote in 181 A D another classic ust as Sugruta did here There is a similarity
in the style of both Detailed description based on experimental work or on personal
observation, characterizes the writings of both of them In the fourth century
Ousbasius of Pergamon ( 825 A D ) wrote books presenting Hippocrates and
Galen in popular forms just as Vagbhata did in the case of Charaka and Sugiuta in
Inca After the seventh century, western civihzation suffered a set-back and there
was a general decline in art and science In India, this stage of things was to come
some six o1 seven centunies later The renaissance o1 the so called scientific age in
Europe began with the period between the fourteenth and sixteenth centuries

Tt was the 1evival of arts and letters under the influence of classical methods in the
countries of 1ts progiess

The European ienaissance gave the western world the modern scientific
spirit. This scientific spirrt which is the basie principle of the present civihzation
js the influence of the great movement of the renaissance.

THE REVIVAL OF LEARNING

This gieat movement known as the Renaissance or Revival of Learning can
be considered ;here only as 1egaids its influence on medicine It influenced medical
science m many ways fist by the revival of the Greek medical classics in the
original, which, though these writers had been imperfectly and in very inaccmate
versions known before, was found to breathe a new spirit into medical learning,
next by the mvention of printmg, which came at the right time to permit the
works rediscovered by scholars to be widely distubuted, and above all by the
renewal of intellectual activity, the bold challengmg of authouty and the spuit of
inquiry and research, which were the distinguishing marks of the New Learning

Moreover, the new scientific methods found new objects to work upon, which the
old systems were incompetent to explain

The first step taken was the revival of the Greek medical classics The Gueek
mannsmnpls we1e collected fiom all ove1 the countries of Europe, Afiica and Asia
al a vere translated in Latin The pioneers in these efforts were scholars physicians
ani publishers Works of Hippocrates, Galen, Dioscorides, Celsus and other medical
cassie ea ‘omed into Lat and often they were printed with orgmal Greek
vended ee at enthusiasm prevailed at that period for such wok, and xt is

the printing presses of Europe, in the fifteenth century issued about

forty thousand incunabula Eadl
] h edition averaged shout five bi
thus twenty milhons of books were printed in all. sndhed copes and

It was a Grecian Cc .
1 a more spacious 1eslm ad Greek medical literature revived and expanded

she had ever known,

=== PAGE 8 ===

{ 5]

The publisher Aldus Manutius has played an important role in this work of
Renaissance. His motto was ‘never to allow scholars to want for good books of
literature and science’, He cairied his enterprise unobstructed by poverty or
pohtical interferences. His was the house where scholars and savants and physicians
crowded for help, co operation and guidance. He died poor but he rendered the
yeoman service by rescuing the priceless works on arts and sciences of ancient
Greeks and so his name will ever be remembered by fature generations

Thus what has been achieved as a result of renaissance in Europe and the
means by which it was fulfilled, cin well serve us as guidance and an example for
the advancement of our knowledge, culture and learning.

It is with this thing in view that our oldest and authoritative classics of
medicine—Caraka and Snéruta, need hundreds of editions together with their
translations and commentaries By doing this, in the first place the faults and
defects in the original texts will be corrected and removed Our scriptures and
treatises of science, coming as they are from manuscripts, bear many mistakes and
changes of form in the process of writing fiom one scribe to another Alberuni the
great muslim scholar of the 11th century says in this connection:

“Besides the faults of the translators a considerable corruption of the text
by the negligence of the copyists more particularly as regards the proper
name is found.

The Indian scribes are careless, and do not take pains to produce correct. and
well-collated copies in consequence, the highest results of the author's mental
development are lost by their negligence and his book becomes already in the
first or second copy so full of faults, that the text appears as something entirely
new which neither a scholar nor one familar with the subject, whether Hindu or
Muslim could any longer understand. It will sufficiently illustrate the matter if
we tell the reader that we have sometimes written down a word from the mouth
of Hindus, taking the greatest pains to fix its pronunciation and that afterwards
when we repeated it to them, they had great difficulty in recognising it”

Moreover, each commentary may throw some light on many significant and
potent descriptions which need more extensive and lucid interpretations to be

clearly understood in this age.

So the aim of each commentator is to simplify and reveal the latent signifi-

~ cance of the text. Now Suéruta Samhita is a treatise of universal importance It
is one of the Vedas of the Science of Medicine in India and so various commentators
from various countries have written commentaries on it according to each one’s
predilection There are translations of Suéruta in various languages both of the
East and the West. Yet it isnot only desirable but essential to have as many

=== PAGE 9 ===

[ 6 ]

translations and commentaries as possible for the enlightenment and elucidation
of the classical work on surgery like Sugruta Samhita

It is with this view that I welcome this present publication which has been
brought out by the paimstaking Kaviray Ambikaduttayi who has tried his best
to make the work intelligible and simple I congratulate the author for the onerous
task that he has fulfilled so energetically and patiently. The publishers also deserve
praise and compliment for their helpful attitude, encouragement and support in this
publication

T express my indebtedness and cordial thanks both to the author and the
publisher who so affectionately invited me to accept the honom of writing this
foreword

JAMNAGAR
Dt 7-8-1953 P M. MEHTA
